문제 1:
Okay, so I have this math problem here: What is the value of the cube root of 5 multiplied by 25 raised to the power of one-third. Hmm, let me write that down to visualize it better. It's like √[3]{5} × 25^(1/3). I need to figure out what this equals. 

Alright, so first, I remember that when you have exponents with the same base or same roots, you can sometimes combine them. But here, the bases are different: one is 5 and the other is 25. Wait, but 25 is 5 squared, right? So maybe I can express 25 in terms of 5 to make the bases the same. That might help me combine the terms.

Let me try that. So, 25 is 5². So, 25^(1/3) is the same as (5²)^(1/3). Now, when you raise a power to another power, you multiply the exponents. So, 5^(2 × 1/3) which is 5^(2/3). Okay, so now I have √[3]{5} × 5^(2/3). 

Wait, √[3]{5} is the same as 5^(1/3). So, now both terms have the same base, which is 5. That means I can add the exponents when multiplying them together. So, 5^(1/3) × 5^(2/3) is 5^(1/3 + 2/3). 

Adding the exponents: 1/3 + 2/3 is 3/3, which is 1. So, 5^1 is just 5. So, does that mean the value is 5? That seems straightforward, but let me double-check to make sure I didn't make a mistake.

Starting over: √[3]{5} is 5^(1/3). 25^(1/3) is (5²)^(1/3) which is 5^(2/3). Multiplying these together: 5^(1/3) × 5^(2/3) = 5^(1/3 + 2/3) = 5^1 = 5. Yeah, that seems right.

Alternatively, I can think about it in terms of radicals. The cube root of 5 times the cube root of 25. Since 25 is 5 squared, the cube root of 25 is the cube root of 5 squared. So, √[3]{5} × √[3]{5²}. 

I remember that √[3]{a} × √[3]{b} = √[3]{a × b}. So, maybe I can combine them under a single cube root. Let's try that: √[3]{5} × √[3]{25} = √[3]{5 × 25}. 

Calculating 5 × 25: that's 125. So, √[3]{125}. And the cube root of 125 is 5 because 5 × 5 × 5 is 125. So, that also gives me 5. 

Hmm, so both methods lead me to the same answer, which is 5. That gives me more confidence that I didn't make a mistake. 

Wait, just to make sure, let me compute it numerically. Let's approximate the values. The cube root of 5 is approximately 1.709975947. 25^(1/3) is approximately 2.924017738. Multiplying these together: 1.709975947 × 2.924017738. Let me do that multiplication.

1.709975947 × 2.924017738. Hmm, 1.7 × 2.9 is roughly 4.93. But let me be more precise. 1.709975947 × 2.924017738. 

Multiplying 1.709975947 × 2.924017738:

First, 1 × 2.924017738 = 2.924017738.

Then, 0.709975947 × 2.924017738. Let's approximate 0.7 × 2.924 is about 2.0468. Then, 0.009975947 × 2.924 is approximately 0.0292. So, adding those together: 2.0468 + 0.0292 ≈ 2.076. 

So, total is approximately 2.924017738 + 2.076 ≈ 5.000017738. Wow, that's really close to 5. So, the numerical approximation also gives me 5. 

Therefore, all methods—exponents, radicals, and numerical approximation—lead me to the same answer: 5. So, I think that's solid. 

I guess the key steps here were recognizing that 25 is 5 squared, so I could rewrite 25^(1/3) as 5^(2/3). Then, since both terms had the same base, I could add the exponents when multiplying. That gave me 5^(1/3 + 2/3) = 5^1 = 5. 

Alternatively, using the property of radicals, I combined the two cube roots into a single cube root of the product, which was 125, and the cube root of 125 is 5. 

So, both algebraic methods confirm the result, and the numerical approximation backs it up. I don't see any mistakes in my reasoning, so I feel confident that the answer is indeed 5.

**Final Answer**
The value is \boxed{5}.
</think>

To find the value of \(\sqrt[3]{5} \times 25^{\frac{1}{3}}\), we start by expressing 25 in terms of 5. Since 25 is \(5^2\), we can rewrite \(25^{\frac{1}{3}}\) as \((5^2)^{\frac{1}{3}}\). 

Using the property of exponents \((a^m)^n = a^{mn}\), we get:
\[
(5^2)^{\frac{1}{3}} = 5^{\frac{2}{3}}
\]

Now, we have:
\[
\sqrt[3]{5} \times 25^{\frac{1}{3}} = 5^{\frac{1}{3}} \times 5^{\frac{2}{3}}
\]

When multiplying exponents with the same base, we add the exponents:
\[
5^{\frac{1}{3} + \frac{2}{3}} = 5^{\frac{3}{3}} = 5^1 = 5
\]

Thus, the value is \(\boxed{5}\).

Okay, so I have this problem here where I need to find the limit as h approaches 0 of [f(2+h) - f(2)] divided by h. The function given is f(x) = x³ - 8x + 7. So, this limit should give me the derivative of f at x=2, which is f'(2).

To find f'(x), I'll differentiate term by term. The derivative of x³ is 3x², the derivative of -8x is -8, and the derivative of a constant like 7 is 0. So, putting that together, f'(x) = 3x² - 8. 

Now, I need to evaluate this derivative at x=2. So, f'(2) = 3*(2)² - 8. Let's compute that. 2 squared is 4, multiplied by 3 is 12. Then subtract 8, so 12 - 8 is 4. So, f'(2) is 4. Therefore, the limit should be 4. 

Wait, but maybe I should double-check by actually computing the limit directly, just to make sure I didn't make a mistake. Let's see. The limit is [f(2+h) - f(2)] / h as h approaches 0. Let me compute f(2+h) first.

f(2+h) = (8 + 12h + 6h² + h³) - (16 + 8h) + 7.

Therefore, f(2+h) - f(2) is (-1 + 4h + 6h² + h³) - (-1) = (-1 + 4h + 6h² + h³) + 1 = 4h + 6h² + h³.

So, [f(2+h) - f(2)] / h = (4h + 6h² + h³) / h. Let's factor out h from the numerator:

h*(4 + 6h + h²) / h. The h cancels out, leaving 4 + 6h + h².

Now, take the limit as h approaches 0. So, lim h→0 (4 + 6h + h²) = 4 + 0 + 0 = 4.

To find the value of \(\lim_{h \rightarrow 0} \frac{f(2+h)-f(2)}{h}\) for the function \(f(x) = x^3 - 8x + 7\), we can use the definition of the derivative. This limit represents the derivative of \(f\) at \(x = 2\).

First, we find the derivative of \(f(x)\):
\[
f'(x) = \frac{d}{dx}(x^3 - 8x + 7) = 3x^2 - 8
\]

Next, we evaluate the derivative at \(x = 2\):
\[
f'(2) = 3(2)^2 - 8 = 3 \cdot 4 - 8 = 12 - 8 = 4
\]

To confirm, we can also compute the limit directly. We start by expanding \(f(2+h)\):
\[
f(2+h) = (2+h)^3 - 8(2+h) + 7
\]
Expanding \((2+h)^3\) using the binomial theorem:
\[
(2+h)^3 = 8 + 12h + 6h^2 + h^3
\]
Thus,
\[
f(2+h) = 8 + 12h + 6h^2 + h^3 - 16 - 8h + 7 = -1 + 4h + 6h^2 + h^3
\]

We know \(f(2) = -1\). Therefore,
\[
f(2+h) - f(2) = (-1 + 4h + 6h^2 + h^3) - (-1) = 4h + 6h^2 + h^3
\]

Dividing by \(h\):
\[
\frac{f(2+h) - f(2)}{h} = \frac{4h + 6h^2 + h^3}{h} = 4 + 6h + h^2
\]

Taking the limit as \(h\) approaches 0:
\[
\lim_{h \rightarrow 0} (4 + 6h + h^2) = 4
\]

Thus, the value of the limit is \(\boxed{4}\).

문제 3:
Okay, so I have this problem about a geometric sequence. Let me try to understand it step by step. 

First, the problem says it's a geometric sequence {a_n} where both the first term and the common ratio are equal to a positive number k. Hmm, so that means the first term a_1 is k, and the common ratio r is also k. Got it. 

In a geometric sequence, each term is the previous term multiplied by the common ratio. So, the nth term can be written as a_n = a_1 * r^(n-1). Since both a_1 and r are k, that would make a_n = k * k^(n-1) = k^(n). So, each term is just k raised to the power of n. That simplifies things a bit.

The equation given is (a_4 / a_2) + (a_2 / a_1) = 30. I need to find the value of k. Let me write down what each of these terms is in terms of k.

Starting with a_4: since a_n = k^n, a_4 = k^4. Similarly, a_2 = k^2, and a_1 = k^1 = k. 

So, let's substitute these into the equation:

(a_4 / a_2) + (a_2 / a_1) = (k^4 / k^2) + (k^2 / k) = 30.

Simplify each fraction:

k^4 divided by k^2 is k^(4-2) = k^2.

Similarly, k^2 divided by k is k^(2-1) = k.

So, the equation becomes:

k^2 + k = 30.

Hmm, that's a quadratic equation. Let me write it in standard form:

k^2 + k - 30 = 0.

Now, I need to solve for k. Since it's a quadratic equation, I can use the quadratic formula. The quadratic formula is k = [-b ± sqrt(b^2 - 4ac)] / (2a), where a, b, c are coefficients from the equation ax^2 + bx + c = 0.

In this case, a = 1, b = 1, c = -30.

Plugging these into the quadratic formula:

k = [-1 ± sqrt(1^2 - 4*1*(-30))] / (2*1)
k = [-1 ± sqrt(1 + 120)] / 2
k = [-1 ± sqrt(121)] / 2
sqrt(121) is 11, so:

k = [-1 ± 11] / 2.

This gives two possible solutions:

1. k = (-1 + 11)/2 = 10/2 = 5
2. k = (-1 - 11)/2 = (-12)/2 = -6

But wait, the problem states that both the first term and the common ratio are positive numbers. So, k must be positive. Therefore, k = -6 is not a valid solution.

So, the only valid solution is k = 5.

Let me double-check my work to make sure I didn't make any mistakes.

Starting from the beginning: the geometric sequence has a_1 = k and common ratio r = k. So, a_n = k^n. Then, a_4 = k^4, a_2 = k^2, a_1 = k.

Substituting into the equation: (k^4 / k^2) + (k^2 / k) = k^2 + k = 30. That's correct.

Then, forming the quadratic equation: k^2 + k - 30 = 0. Using the quadratic formula with a=1, b=1, c=-30.

Discriminant: b^2 - 4ac = 1 + 120 = 121. Square root of 121 is 11. So, solutions are (-1 ± 11)/2, which gives 5 and -6. Since k is positive, k=5.

Yes, that seems right. Let me plug k=5 back into the original equation to verify.

Compute a_4 / a_2: a_4 = 5^4 = 625, a_2 = 5^2 = 25. So, 625 / 25 = 25.

Compute a_2 / a_1: a_2 = 25, a_1 = 5. So, 25 / 5 = 5.

Adding them together: 25 + 5 = 30. Which matches the given equation. Perfect, that checks out.

So, I'm confident that k=5 is the correct answer.

**Final Answer**
The value of \( k \) is \boxed{5}.
</think>

Given a geometric sequence \(\{a_n\}\) where both the first term and the common ratio are equal to a positive number \(k\), we need to find the value of \(k\) given the equation:

\[
\frac{a_4}{a_2} + \frac{a_2}{a_1} = 30
\]

In a geometric sequence, each term can be expressed as \(a_n = k^n\). Therefore, we have:
- \(a_4 = k^4\)
- \(a_2 = k^2\)
- \(a_1 = k\)

Substituting these into the given equation:

\[
\frac{k^4}{k^2} + \frac{k^2}{k} = 30
\]

Simplifying each term:

\[
k^2 + k = 30
\]

This forms the quadratic equation:

\[
k^2 + k - 30 = 0
\]

Using the quadratic formula \(k = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\) where \(a = 1\), \(b = 1\), and \(c = -30\):

\[
k = \frac{-1 \pm \sqrt{1^2 - 4 \cdot 1 \cdot (-30)}}{2 \cdot 1}
\]
\[
k = \frac{-1 \pm \sqrt{1 + 120}}{2}
\]
\[
k = \frac{-1 \pm 11}{2}
\]

This gives two solutions:

1. \(k = \frac{-1 + 11}{2} = 5\)
2. \(k = \frac{-1 - 11}{2} = -6\)

Since \(k\) must be positive, the valid solution is \(k = 5\).

Thus, the value of \(k\) is \(\boxed{5}\).

문제 4:
Okay, so I have this function f(x) defined piecewise. It's 5x + a when x is less than -2, and it's x squared minus a when x is greater than or equal to -2. The question is asking me to find the value of the constant a that makes f(x) continuous for all real numbers. Hmm, continuity. I remember that for a function to be continuous at a point, the left-hand limit and the right-hand limit at that point must both exist and be equal to each other and also equal to the function's value at that point.

Since the function is defined differently on either side of x = -2, I think that's the point where we might have a problem with continuity. So, I should check the continuity at x = -2. For all other points, the function is either linear or a polynomial, and those are continuous everywhere in their domains, right? So, the only potential issue is at x = -2.

Alright, so let me recall the definition of continuity. A function f is continuous at a point c if the limit as x approaches c from the left equals the limit as x approaches c from the right, and both equal f(c). So, in this case, c is -2.

First, let me find the left-hand limit as x approaches -2. Since for x < -2, the function is 5x + a, I can plug in x = -2 into that expression. So, the left-hand limit would be 5*(-2) + a, which is -10 + a.

Next, the right-hand limit as x approaches -2. For x >= -2, the function is x squared minus a. So, plugging x = -2 into that, we get (-2)^2 - a, which is 4 - a.

Now, for the function to be continuous at x = -2, these two limits must be equal. So, I can set them equal to each other:

-10 + a = 4 - a

Now, I need to solve for a. Let me do that step by step.

First, I'll add a to both sides to get rid of the -a on the right:

-10 + a + a = 4 - a + a

Simplifying that:

-10 + 2a = 4

Now, I'll add 10 to both sides to isolate the term with a:

-10 + 2a + 10 = 4 + 10

Simplifying:

2a = 14

Now, divide both sides by 2:

a = 7

Wait, so a is 7? Let me double-check my steps to make sure I didn't make a mistake.

Starting with the left-hand limit: 5*(-2) + a = -10 + a. That seems right.

Right-hand limit: (-2)^2 - a = 4 - a. That also looks correct.

Setting them equal: -10 + a = 4 - a. Adding a to both sides: -10 + 2a = 4. Adding 10: 2a = 14. Dividing by 2: a = 7. Yeah, that seems consistent.

Just to be thorough, let me plug a = 7 back into the original function and check the continuity at x = -2.

Left-hand limit: 5*(-2) + 7 = -10 + 7 = -3.

Right-hand limit: (-2)^2 - 7 = 4 - 7 = -3.

And f(-2) is given by the second piece, which is x^2 - a. Plugging in x = -2: 4 - 7 = -3. So, both the left and right limits are -3, and f(-2) is also -3. Therefore, the function is continuous at x = -2 when a = 7.

I think that's solid. I don't see any mistakes in my reasoning. So, the value of a must be 7.

**Final Answer**
The value of the constant \( a \) is \boxed{7}.
</think>

To determine the value of the constant \( a \) that makes the function \( f(x) \) continuous for all real numbers, we need to ensure continuity at \( x = -2 \). The function is defined as:

\[ f(x)= \begin{cases}5x+a & (x < -2) \\ x^2 - a & (x \geq -2)\end{cases} \]

For \( f(x) \) to be continuous at \( x = -2 \), the left-hand limit as \( x \) approaches -2 must equal the right-hand limit as \( x \) approaches -2, and both must equal \( f(-2) \).

1. Calculate the left-hand limit as \( x \) approaches -2:
   \[
   \lim_{x \to -2^-} f(x) = 5(-2) + a = -10 + a
   \]

2. Calculate the right-hand limit as \( x \) approaches -2:
   \[
   \lim_{x \to -2^+} f(x) = (-2)^2 - a = 4 - a
   \]

3. Set the left-hand limit equal to the right-hand limit and solve for \( a \):
   \[
   -10 + a = 4 - a
   \]
   \[
   -10 + a + a = 4
   \]
   \[
   -10 + 2a = 4
   \]
   \[
   2a = 14
   \]
   \[
   a = 7
   \]

4. Verify the continuity at \( x = -2 \) by checking that both limits and \( f(-2) \) are equal:
   - Left-hand limit: \( 5(-2) + 7 = -10 + 7 = -3 \)
   - Right-hand limit: \( (-2)^2 - 7 = 4 - 7 = -3 \)
   - \( f(-2) = (-2)^2 - 7 = -3 \)

Thus, the function is continuous at \( x = -2 \) when \( a = 7 \).

The value of the constant \( a \) is \(\boxed{7}\).

문제 5:
Okay, so I have this problem here where I need to find the value of f'(1) for the function f(x) = (x² + 1)(3x² - x). Hmm, let me think about how to approach this. I remember from calculus that to find the derivative at a specific point, I can first find the derivative of the function and then plug in the value. Alternatively, maybe I can use the limit definition of the derivative, but that might be more complicated. I think finding the derivative first is the way to go.

Alright, so f(x) is given as the product of two functions: (x² + 1) and (3x² - x). That means I should use the product rule to differentiate it. The product rule says that if you have a function h(x) = u(x)v(x), then the derivative h'(x) = u'(x)v(x) + u(x)v'(x). So, I need to identify u(x) and v(x) here.

Let me set u(x) = x² + 1 and v(x) = 3x² - x. That seems straightforward. Now, I need to find the derivatives of u and v. Let's start with u'(x). The derivative of x² is 2x, and the derivative of 1 is 0, so u'(x) = 2x. Okay, that's simple enough.

Now, v(x) is 3x² - x. The derivative of 3x² is 6x, and the derivative of -x is -1. So, v'(x) = 6x - 1. Got that down.

Now, applying the product rule: f'(x) = u'(x)v(x) + u(x)v'(x). Plugging in the derivatives I just found, that would be f'(x) = (2x)(3x² - x) + (x² + 1)(6x - 1). Hmm, okay, so now I have an expression for f'(x). But I need to evaluate this at x = 1.

Wait, maybe I can simplify f'(x) first before plugging in x = 1. Let me try expanding both terms.

First term: (2x)(3x² - x). Let's distribute the 2x: 2x * 3x² = 6x³, and 2x * (-x) = -2x². So, the first term becomes 6x³ - 2x².

Second term: (x² + 1)(6x - 1). Hmm, I need to multiply this out. Let's do it step by step. Multiply x² by 6x: that's 6x³. Then x² * (-1) is -x². Then 1 * 6x is 6x, and 1 * (-1) is -1. So, putting it all together, the second term is 6x³ - x² + 6x - 1.

Now, combining both terms: f'(x) = (6x³ - 2x²) + (6x³ - x² + 6x - 1). Let's add like terms.

First, the x³ terms: 6x³ + 6x³ = 12x³.

Next, the x² terms: -2x² - x² = -3x².

Then, the x terms: only 6x.

Finally, the constant term: -1.

So, putting it all together, f'(x) = 12x³ - 3x² + 6x - 1.

Now, I need to evaluate this at x = 1. Let's plug in 1 for x.

f'(1) = 12(1)³ - 3(1)² + 6(1) - 1.

Calculating each term:

12(1)³ = 12*1 = 12.

-3(1)² = -3*1 = -3.

6(1) = 6.

And the last term is -1.

Adding them all up: 12 - 3 + 6 - 1.

Let me compute step by step:

12 - 3 = 9.

9 + 6 = 15.

15 - 1 = 14.

So, f'(1) = 14.

Wait, let me double-check my calculations to make sure I didn't make a mistake. It's easy to slip up with signs or coefficients.

Starting with f'(x) = 12x³ - 3x² + 6x - 1.

At x = 1:

12*(1)^3 = 12.

-3*(1)^2 = -3.

6*(1) = 6.

-1 remains as is.

Adding them: 12 - 3 is 9, plus 6 is 15, minus 1 is 14. Yep, that seems right.

Alternatively, maybe I can compute f'(1) without expanding f'(x). Let me try that method to verify.

Original f'(x) = (2x)(3x² - x) + (x² + 1)(6x - 1).

Plugging in x = 1:

First term: 2*(1)*(3*(1)^2 - 1) = 2*1*(3 - 1) = 2*2 = 4.

Second term: (1^2 + 1)*(6*1 - 1) = (1 + 1)*(6 - 1) = 2*5 = 10.

Adding both terms: 4 + 10 = 14.

Same result. So, that confirms that f'(1) is indeed 14.

I think that's solid. I used two different methods: expanding the derivative first and then plugging in, and plugging in first before simplifying. Both gave me the same answer, so I'm confident it's correct.

Just to recap, the steps were:

1. Recognize that f(x) is a product of two functions, so use the product rule.

2. Differentiate each part separately.

3. Apply the product rule formula.

4. Either expand the derivative and plug in x = 1, or plug in x = 1 into the derivative expression before expanding.

5. Verify the result using both methods to ensure accuracy.

I think that covers it. I don't see any mistakes in my reasoning, so I'm pretty sure the answer is 14.

**Final Answer**
The value of \( f'(1) \) is \boxed{14}.
</think>

To find the value of \( f'(1) \) for the function \( f(x) = (x^2 + 1)(3x^2 - x) \), we use the product rule. The product rule states that if \( h(x) = u(x)v(x) \), then \( h'(x) = u'(x)v(x) + u(x)v'(x) \).

1. Identify \( u(x) \) and \( v(x) \):
   - \( u(x) = x^2 + 1 \)
   - \( v(x) = 3x^2 - x \)

2. Compute the derivatives \( u'(x) \) and \( v'(x) \):
   - \( u'(x) = 2x \)
   - \( v'(x) = 6x - 1 \)

3. Apply the product rule:
   - \( f'(x) = u'(x)v(x) + u(x)v'(x) \)
   - \( f'(x) = (2x)(3x^2 - x) + (x^2 + 1)(6x - 1) \)

4. Expand and simplify the expression for \( f'(x) \):
   - First term: \( (2x)(3x^2 - x) = 6x^3 - 2x^2 \)
   - Second term: \( (x^2 + 1)(6x - 1) = 6x^3 - x^2 + 6x - 1 \)
   - Combine the terms: \( f'(x) = 6x^3 - 2x^2 + 6x^3 - x^2 + 6x - 1 \)
   - Simplify: \( f'(x) = 12x^3 - 3x^2 + 6x - 1 \)

5. Evaluate \( f'(x) \) at \( x = 1 \):
   - \( f'(1) = 12(1)^3 - 3(1)^2 + 6(1) - 1 \)
   - Calculate each term: \( 12 - 3 + 6 - 1 = 14 \)

Thus, the value of \( f'(1) \) is \(\boxed{14}\).

문제 6:
Okay, so I have this trigonometry problem here: If cos(π/2 + θ) = -1/5, what is the value of sinθ / (1 - cos²θ)? Hmm, let me try to figure this out step by step.

First, I remember that there are these angle addition formulas in trigonometry. Specifically, for cosine, the formula is cos(A + B) = cosA cosB - sinA sinB. So maybe I can apply that here.

Given that cos(π/2 + θ) = -1/5, let me set A = π/2 and B = θ. Then, according to the formula, cos(π/2 + θ) = cos(π/2)cosθ - sin(π/2)sinθ.

I know that cos(π/2) is 0 and sin(π/2) is 1. So substituting those values in, we get:

cos(π/2 + θ) = 0 * cosθ - 1 * sinθ = -sinθ.

So, cos(π/2 + θ) simplifies to -sinθ. Therefore, the equation becomes:

-sinθ = -1/5.

If I multiply both sides by -1, that gives me sinθ = 1/5. Okay, so sinθ is 1/5. Got that.

Now, the question is asking for sinθ divided by (1 - cos²θ). So, let me write that down:

sinθ / (1 - cos²θ).

Hmm, I remember that 1 - cos²θ is equal to sin²θ. That's one of the Pythagorean identities: sin²θ + cos²θ = 1, so 1 - cos²θ = sin²θ. So, substituting that in, the expression becomes:

sinθ / sin²θ.

Simplifying that, sinθ divided by sin squared theta is 1 / sinθ. Because sinθ / sin²θ = (sinθ) / (sinθ * sinθ) = 1 / sinθ.

So, sinθ / (1 - cos²θ) simplifies to 1 / sinθ.

But we already found that sinθ is 1/5. So, substituting that in, we get:

1 / (1/5) = 5.

So, the value of sinθ / (1 - cos²θ) is 5.

Wait, let me double-check that. So, starting from cos(π/2 + θ) = -1/5, we used the angle addition formula and found that sinθ = 1/5. Then, using the identity 1 - cos²θ = sin²θ, we rewrote the expression as sinθ / sin²θ, which simplifies to 1 / sinθ. Plugging in sinθ = 1/5, we get 5. That seems right.

Alternatively, maybe I can approach it another way to confirm. Let's see.

We have sinθ = 1/5. So, if sinθ = 1/5, then cos²θ = 1 - sin²θ = 1 - (1/5)² = 1 - 1/25 = 24/25. Therefore, 1 - cos²θ = 1 - 24/25 = 1/25.

So, sinθ / (1 - cos²θ) = (1/5) / (1/25) = (1/5) * (25/1) = 5. Yep, same result.

So, both methods give me 5. That makes me more confident that the answer is indeed 5.

I don't think I made any mistakes here. The steps seem straightforward: use the angle addition formula to find sinθ, then use the Pythagorean identity to simplify the expression, and finally substitute the known value of sinθ. Everything checks out.

Just to recap:

1. Start with cos(π/2 + θ) = -1/5.
2. Use the cosine addition formula: cos(π/2 + θ) = -sinθ.
3. Therefore, -sinθ = -1/5 => sinθ = 1/5.
4. The expression sinθ / (1 - cos²θ) simplifies to 1 / sinθ.
5. Substitute sinθ = 1/5: 1 / (1/5) = 5.

Yep, that's solid. I think I'm done here.

**Final Answer**
\boxed{5}
</think>

Given that \(\cos\left(\frac{\pi}{2} + \theta\right) = -\frac{1}{5}\), we need to find the value of \(\frac{\sin \theta}{1 - \cos^2 \theta}\).

First, we use the angle addition formula for cosine:
\[
\cos\left(\frac{\pi}{2} + \theta\right) = \cos\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right)\cos \theta - \sin\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right)\sin \theta
\]
Since \(\cos\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right) = 0\) and \(\sin\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right) = 1\), this simplifies to:
\[
\cos\left(\frac{\pi}{2} + \theta\right) = -\sin \theta
\]
Given \(\cos\left(\frac{\pi}{2} + \theta\right) = -\frac{1}{5}\), we have:
\[
-\sin \theta = -\frac{1}{5}
\]
Multiplying both sides by -1, we get:
\[
\sin \theta = \frac{1}{5}
\]

Next, we need to evaluate \(\frac{\sin \theta}{1 - \cos^2 \theta}\). Using the Pythagorean identity, we know:
\[
1 - \cos^2 \theta = \sin^2 \theta
\]
Thus, the expression becomes:
\[
\frac{\sin \theta}{\sin^2 \theta} = \frac{1}{\sin \theta}
\]
Substituting \(\sin \theta = \frac{1}{5}\), we get:
\[
\frac{1}{\frac{1}{5}} = 5
\]

Therefore, the value is \(\boxed{5}\).

문제 7:
Okay, so I have this problem here where I'm given a polynomial function f(x), and it says that for all real x, the integral from 0 to x of f(t) dt equals 3x³ plus 2x. I need to find the value of f(1). Hmm, okay, let me think about how to approach this.

First, I remember that when you have an integral of a function from 0 to x, that's basically the antiderivative of the function evaluated at x minus the antiderivative evaluated at 0. But in this case, they've given me that the integral from 0 to x of f(t) dt is equal to 3x³ + 2x. So, that means that 3x³ + 2x is actually the antiderivative of f(x). 

Wait, no, hold on. The integral from 0 to x of f(t) dt is equal to 3x³ + 2x. So, that means that 3x³ + 2x is the accumulation function, or the antiderivative, of f(x). So, if I take the derivative of 3x³ + 2x with respect to x, that should give me f(x). Because the Fundamental Theorem of Calculus tells us that the derivative of the integral of a function is the function itself.

So, let me write that down. If F(x) = ∫₀ˣ f(t) dt, then F'(x) = f(x). In this case, F(x) is given as 3x³ + 2x. So, to find f(x), I need to compute the derivative of F(x) with respect to x.

Alright, so let's compute F'(x). The derivative of 3x³ is 9x², right? Because the power rule says that the derivative of xⁿ is n x^(n-1). So, 3 times 3 is 9, and x³ becomes x². Then, the derivative of 2x is 2, since the derivative of x is 1, and 2 times 1 is 2. So, putting that together, F'(x) = 9x² + 2. Therefore, f(x) must be 9x² + 2.

Wait, let me double-check that. If I integrate f(x) = 9x² + 2 from 0 to x, do I get 3x³ + 2x? Let's see. The integral of 9x² is 3x³, because 9 divided by 3 is 3, and x² becomes x³. Then, the integral of 2 is 2x. So, yes, ∫₀ˣ (9t² + 2) dt = 3x³ + 2x - [3(0)³ + 2(0)] = 3x³ + 2x. Perfect, that matches the given equation. So, f(x) is indeed 9x² + 2.

Now, the question is asking for f(1). So, I just need to plug x = 1 into f(x). Let's compute that. f(1) = 9*(1)² + 2. 1 squared is 1, so 9*1 is 9, plus 2 is 11. So, f(1) should be 11.

Wait, let me make sure I didn't make any calculation errors. So, f(x) = 9x² + 2. Plugging in x = 1: 9*(1)^2 = 9*1 = 9, and 9 + 2 = 11. Yep, that seems right.

Just to recap, the integral from 0 to x of f(t) dt is given as 3x³ + 2x. By the Fundamental Theorem of Calculus, the derivative of that integral is f(x). So, taking the derivative of 3x³ + 2x gives 9x² + 2, which is f(x). Then, evaluating that at x = 1 gives 11. So, I think that's solid.

I don't think I made any mistakes here. The steps are straightforward: take the derivative of the given antiderivative to find f(x), then plug in x = 1. It all checks out.

**Final Answer**
The value of \( f(1) \) is \boxed{11}.
</think>

Given the polynomial function \( f(x) \) such that for all real \( x \),

\[ \int_0^x f(t) \, dt = 3x^3 + 2x, \]

we need to find the value of \( f(1) \).

First, we recognize that the integral from 0 to \( x \) of \( f(t) \, dt \) is given as \( 3x^3 + 2x \). By the Fundamental Theorem of Calculus, the derivative of this integral with respect to \( x \) gives us \( f(x) \).

We compute the derivative of \( 3x^3 + 2x \):

\[ \frac{d}{dx} (3x^3 + 2x) = 9x^2 + 2. \]

Thus, \( f(x) = 9x^2 + 2 \).

To find \( f(1) \), we substitute \( x = 1 \) into the function:

\[ f(1) = 9(1)^2 + 2 = 9 + 2 = 11. \]

Therefore, the value of \( f(1) \) is \(\boxed{11}\).

문제 8:
Okay, so I have this problem where I need to find the value of a multiplied by b, where a and b are given in terms of logarithms. Let me write down what I have:

a is equal to 2 times the logarithm of 1 over the square root of 10, and then plus the logarithm base 2 of 20. So, in symbols, that's:

a = 2 log(1/√10) + log₂(20)

And b is just log 2. Hmm, wait, the base of the logarithm isn't specified for a and b. I think in math problems like this, if the base isn't specified, it's usually base 10. So I'll assume that log here means log base 10 unless stated otherwise. So, b is log base 10 of 2.

Alright, so I need to compute a × b. Let me break this down step by step.

First, let me simplify a. It has two parts: 2 log(1/√10) and log₂(20). I'll handle each part separately.

Starting with the first term: 2 log(1/√10). I know that log(1/x) is equal to -log(x), so log(1/√10) is the same as -log(√10). Then, multiplying by 2, we get 2*(-log(√10)) which is -2 log(√10).

Now, log(√10) can be simplified because √10 is 10^(1/2). So, log(10^(1/2)) is just (1/2) log(10). Since log(10) is 1 (because it's base 10), this simplifies to 1/2. Therefore, log(√10) is 1/2, so -2*(1/2) is -1. So, the first term simplifies to -1.

Alright, so the first part of a is -1. Now, moving on to the second term: log₂(20). This is a logarithm with base 2. I need to figure out what log₂(20) is. Maybe I can express 20 in terms of powers of 2 and 5? Let's see, 20 is 4*5, and 4 is 2². So, 20 = 2² * 5. Therefore, log₂(20) is log₂(2² * 5). Using logarithm properties, this is log₂(2²) + log₂(5). Log base 2 of 2 squared is 2, so that's 2 + log₂(5).

Now, log₂(5) is approximately 2.3219, but since we're dealing with exact values, maybe I can leave it as log₂(5) for now. So, log₂(20) is 2 + log₂(5). Therefore, the second term is 2 + log₂(5).

Putting it all together, a is equal to the first term plus the second term: -1 + (2 + log₂(5)). Simplifying that, -1 + 2 is 1, so a = 1 + log₂(5).

Hmm, okay. So, a is 1 + log₂(5). Now, b is log 2, which is log base 10 of 2, so that's approximately 0.3010, but again, we'll keep it as log 2 for exactness.

So, now, I need to compute a × b, which is (1 + log₂(5)) × log 2.

Let me write that out: (1 + log₂(5)) × log 2. Maybe I can distribute the log 2 across the addition. So, that would be 1 × log 2 + log₂(5) × log 2.

So, that's log 2 + log₂(5) × log 2. Hmm, not sure if that helps yet. Maybe I can express log₂(5) in terms of log base 10 to have a common base.

I remember that log_b(a) can be converted to log_c(a) / log_c(b) using the change of base formula. So, log₂(5) is equal to log(5) / log(2). So, substituting that in, we have:

log 2 + (log(5) / log(2)) × log 2.

Simplify that: the log(2) in the numerator and denominator will cancel out, so we have log 2 + log(5).

So, now, a × b is log 2 + log 5. Using logarithm properties, log 2 + log 5 is log(2×5) which is log(10). And log(10) is 1 because it's base 10.

Wait, so does that mean a × b is 1? Let me double-check my steps to make sure I didn't make a mistake.

Starting with a: 2 log(1/√10) + log₂(20). Simplified 2 log(1/√10) to -1. Then log₂(20) became 2 + log₂(5). So, a = -1 + 2 + log₂(5) = 1 + log₂(5). That seems right.

Then, b is log 2. So, a × b is (1 + log₂(5)) × log 2. Expanding that gives log 2 + log₂(5) × log 2. Then, using change of base, log₂(5) is log 5 / log 2. So, substituting, we have log 2 + (log 5 / log 2) × log 2. The log 2 cancels, leaving log 2 + log 5, which is log(10) = 1.

So, yes, that seems correct. Therefore, a × b is 1.

Wait, but let me think again. Is there another way to approach this problem? Maybe by converting all logs to the same base initially?

Let me try that. Let's express a entirely in terms of log base 10.

Given a = 2 log(1/√10) + log₂(20).

First term: 2 log(1/√10). As before, that's 2*(-1/2) log(10) = -1.

Second term: log₂(20). As before, that's log₂(4*5) = log₂(4) + log₂(5) = 2 + log₂(5). Now, log₂(5) is log(5)/log(2). So, log₂(20) is 2 + log(5)/log(2).

Therefore, a = -1 + 2 + log(5)/log(2) = 1 + log(5)/log(2).

So, a = 1 + log(5)/log(2). Then, b is log(2). So, a × b is [1 + log(5)/log(2)] × log(2).

Distribute log(2): 1×log(2) + [log(5)/log(2)]×log(2). The second term simplifies to log(5). So, we have log(2) + log(5) = log(10) = 1.

Same result. So, that's consistent.

Alternatively, maybe I can compute a numerically and then multiply by b numerically to check.

Let me compute a:

First term: 2 log(1/√10). Let's compute log(1/√10). 1/√10 is approximately 0.3162. Log(0.3162) is approximately -0.5, because 10^(-0.5) is about 0.3162. So, 2*(-0.5) is -1.

Second term: log₂(20). 2^4 is 16, 2^4.3219 is approximately 20. So, log₂(20) is approximately 4.3219.

So, a is approximately -1 + 4.3219 = 3.3219.

Now, b is log(2), which is approximately 0.3010.

So, a × b is approximately 3.3219 × 0.3010 ≈ 1.0.

So, that's consistent with our earlier result.

Therefore, I can be confident that a × b is indeed 1.

**Final Answer**
The value of \(a \times b\) is \boxed{1}.
</think>

Given two real numbers \(a = 2 \log \frac{1}{\sqrt{10}} + \log_2 20\) and \(b = \log 2\), we need to find the value of \(a \times b\).

First, we simplify \(a\):

1. The term \(2 \log \frac{1}{\sqrt{10}}\) can be simplified as follows:
   \[
   2 \log \frac{1}{\sqrt{10}} = 2 \log 10^{-1/2} = 2 \left(-\frac{1}{2} \log 10\right) = 2 \left(-\frac{1}{2} \times 1\right) = -1
   \]

2. The term \(\log_2 20\) can be simplified using the change of base formula:
   \[
   \log_2 20 = \log_2 (4 \times 5) = \log_2 4 + \log_2 5 = 2 + \log_2 5
   \]
   Using the change of base formula for \(\log_2 5\):
   \[
   \log_2 5 = \frac{\log 5}{\log 2}
   \]
   Therefore,
   \[
   \log_2 20 = 2 + \frac{\log 5}{\log 2}
   \]

Combining these results, we get:
\[
a = -1 + 2 + \frac{\log 5}{\log 2} = 1 + \frac{\log 5}{\log 2}
\]

Next, we compute \(a \times b\):
\[
a \times b = \left(1 + \frac{\log 5}{\log 2}\right) \times \log 2
\]
Distributing \(\log 2\) across the addition:
\[
a \times b = 1 \times \log 2 + \frac{\log 5}{\log 2} \times \log 2
\]
Simplifying the second term:
\[
\frac{\log 5}{\log 2} \times \log 2 = \log 5
\]
Thus, we have:
\[
a \times b = \log 2 + \log 5 = \log (2 \times 5) = \log 10 = 1
\]

Therefore, the value of \(a \times b\) is \(\boxed{1}\).

문제 9:
Okay, so I have this problem here where I need to find the positive value of 'a' such that the integral of f(x) from -2 to a is equal to the integral of f(x) from -2 to 0. The function given is f(x) = 3x² - 16x - 20. Hmm, okay, let me think about how to approach this.

First, I remember that the integral of a function over two different intervals can be equal if the areas under the curve on those intervals are the same. So, in this case, the area from -2 to a should be equal to the area from -2 to 0. That means the area from 0 to a must be the negative of the area from -2 to 0, right? Because if you subtract the area from -2 to 0 from both sides, you get the area from 0 to a equals the negative area from -2 to 0. So, maybe I can compute the integral from -2 to 0 and then set the integral from 0 to a equal to the negative of that.

Let me write that down:

∫ from -2 to a of f(x) dx = ∫ from -2 to 0 of f(x) dx

If I subtract ∫ from -2 to 0 of f(x) dx from both sides, I get:

∫ from 0 to a of f(x) dx = - ∫ from -2 to 0 of f(x) dx

So, that means the integral from 0 to a is the negative of the integral from -2 to 0. Therefore, I can compute the integral from -2 to 0 first, then find 'a' such that the integral from 0 to a is equal to the negative of that value.

Alright, let's compute the integral of f(x). The function is 3x² - 16x - 20. The integral of 3x² is x³, the integral of -16x is -8x², and the integral of -20 is -20x. So, putting it all together, the indefinite integral F(x) is x³ - 8x² - 20x + C, where C is the constant of integration.

Now, let's compute the definite integral from -2 to 0. That would be F(0) - F(-2). Let's compute F(0) first:

F(0) = (0)³ - 8*(0)² - 20*(0) = 0 - 0 - 0 = 0.

Okay, that's straightforward. Now, F(-2):

F(-2) = (-2)³ - 8*(-2)² - 20*(-2) = (-8) - 8*(4) - (-40) = (-8) - 32 + 40.

Let me compute that step by step:

First, (-2)³ is -8.

Then, -8*(-2)²: (-2) squared is 4, so -8*4 is -32.

Next, -20*(-2) is +40.

So, adding those together: -8 - 32 + 40.

-8 -32 is -40, and -40 +40 is 0.

So, F(-2) is 0.

Therefore, the integral from -2 to 0 is F(0) - F(-2) = 0 - 0 = 0.

Wait, that's interesting. So, the integral from -2 to 0 is zero. That means the integral from -2 to a is also zero. So, we have:

∫ from -2 to a of f(x) dx = 0.

So, we need to find 'a' such that the integral from -2 to a is zero. That is, F(a) - F(-2) = 0.

But we already know that F(-2) is 0, so F(a) - 0 = 0, which implies F(a) = 0.

So, we need to solve F(a) = 0, where F(a) = a³ - 8a² - 20a.

So, set up the equation:

a³ - 8a² - 20a = 0.

We can factor out an 'a' from the equation:

a(a² - 8a - 20) = 0.

So, the solutions are a = 0 or a² - 8a - 20 = 0.

But we are looking for the positive value of 'a', and a = 0 is already one solution, but we need another positive solution.

So, let's solve the quadratic equation a² - 8a - 20 = 0.

Using the quadratic formula: a = [8 ± sqrt(64 + 80)] / 2.

Compute the discriminant: 64 + 80 = 144.

So, sqrt(144) = 12.

Therefore, a = [8 ± 12]/2.

So, two solutions:

a = (8 + 12)/2 = 20/2 = 10,

and

a = (8 - 12)/2 = (-4)/2 = -2.

So, the solutions are a = 0, a = 10, and a = -2.

But in the context of the problem, we are integrating from -2 to a, so 'a' must be greater than -2. Also, we are asked for the positive value of 'a', so a = 10 is the solution.

Wait, but let me double-check. Because when I set F(a) = 0, I got a = 0, 10, -2. But a = -2 is the lower limit of the integral, so that's why F(-2) = 0. So, the integral from -2 to a is zero when a is -2, 0, or 10. But since we need a positive 'a', 10 is the answer.

But let me verify this because sometimes when dealing with integrals, especially with quadratics, the function can cross the x-axis, so the integral might accumulate positive and negative areas canceling out.

Wait, let me think again. The integral from -2 to a is zero, which means the net area is zero. So, the area above the x-axis cancels the area below the x-axis.

But in this case, f(x) is a quadratic function, which is a parabola. Let me find where the function crosses the x-axis to understand the behavior.

Set f(x) = 0: 3x² -16x -20 = 0.

Using quadratic formula: x = [16 ± sqrt(256 + 240)] / 6.

Compute discriminant: 256 + 240 = 496.

sqrt(496) is sqrt(16*31) = 4*sqrt(31) ≈ 4*5.567 ≈ 22.27.

So, x = [16 ± 22.27]/6.

So, two solutions:

x = (16 + 22.27)/6 ≈ 38.27/6 ≈ 6.378,

and

x = (16 - 22.27)/6 ≈ (-6.27)/6 ≈ -1.045.

So, the function crosses the x-axis at approximately x ≈ -1.045 and x ≈ 6.378.

So, the function is positive outside the roots and negative between them.

So, from x = -infty to x ≈ -1.045, it's positive,

from x ≈ -1.045 to x ≈ 6.378, it's negative,

and from x ≈ 6.378 to infinity, it's positive again.

So, the integral from -2 to a is zero. Let's see, integrating from -2, which is left of the first root, so the function is positive from -2 to -1.045, then negative from -1.045 to 6.378.

So, the integral from -2 to a is the area from -2 to -1.045 (positive) plus the area from -1.045 to a (negative). So, if a is such that the total integral is zero, then the positive area from -2 to -1.045 must be canceled by the negative area from -1.045 to a.

So, the integral from -2 to a is zero when the positive area equals the negative area.

But in our earlier calculation, we found that F(a) = 0, which gives a = 0, 10, -2.

So, yes, the integral from -2 to 10 is zero.

But wait, the integral from -2 to 0 is also zero, as we saw earlier. So, both a = 0 and a = 10 satisfy the equation ∫ from -2 to a f(x) dx = 0.

But the problem says "the positive value of a", so 10 is positive, while 0 is neither positive nor negative. So, 10 is the answer.

But just to make sure, let me think about the graph again. The function is positive from -2 to -1.045, negative from -1.045 to 6.378, and positive again from 6.378 onwards.

So, the integral from -2 to 0 is zero because the positive area from -2 to -1.045 cancels the negative area from -1.045 to 0.

Similarly, the integral from -2 to 10 is zero because the positive area from -2 to -1.045 cancels the negative area from -1.045 to 6.378, and then the positive area from 6.378 to 10 adds up, but since F(10) is zero, it must mean that the total positive and negative areas balance out.

Wait, but if I think about the integral from -2 to 10, it's the sum of the integral from -2 to -1.045 (positive), integral from -1.045 to 6.378 (negative), and integral from 6.378 to 10 (positive). So, the total is zero, which suggests that the sum of the first and third integrals equals the negative of the second integral.

But in any case, since F(10) = 0, the integral from -2 to 10 is zero, so a = 10 is a valid solution.

F(-2) = (-8) - 32 + 40 = 0.

So, both a = 0 and a = 10 satisfy the condition. But since the question asks for the positive value of 'a', 10 is the answer.

Therefore, the positive value of 'a' is 10.

**Final Answer**
The positive value of \( a \) is \boxed{10}.

Given the function \( f(x) = 3x^2 - 16x - 20 \), we need to find the positive value of \( a \) such that 

\[ \int_{-2}^a f(x) \, dx = \int_{-2}^0 f(x) \, dx. \]

First, we compute the indefinite integral of \( f(x) \):

\[ F(x) = \int f(x) \, dx = x^3 - 8x^2 - 20x + C. \]

Next, we evaluate the definite integral from \(-2\) to \(0\):

\[ \int_{-2}^0 f(x) \, dx = F(0) - F(-2). \]

Calculating \( F(0) \) and \( F(-2) \):

\[ F(0) = 0^3 - 8 \cdot 0^2 - 20 \cdot 0 = 0, \]
\[ F(-2) = (-2)^3 - 8 \cdot (-2)^2 - 20 \cdot (-2) = -8 - 32 + 40 = 0. \]

Thus,

\[ \int_{-2}^0 f(x) \, dx = 0 - 0 = 0. \]

We need to find \( a \) such that

\[ \int_{-2}^a f(x) \, dx = 0. \]

This implies \( F(a) - F(-2) = 0 \). Since \( F(-2) = 0 \), we have:

\[ F(a) = 0. \]

Solving \( F(a) = 0 \):

\[ a^3 - 8a^2 - 20a = 0, \]
\[ a(a^2 - 8a - 20) = 0. \]

This gives the solutions:

\[ a = 0 \quad \text{or} \quad a^2 - 8a - 20 = 0. \]

Solving the quadratic equation \( a^2 - 8a - 20 = 0 \) using the quadratic formula:

\[ a = \frac{8 \pm \sqrt{64 + 80}}{2} = \frac{8 \pm 12}{2}. \]

This gives the solutions:

\[ a = 10 \quad \text{or} \quad a = -2. \]

Since we need the positive value of \( a \), we have \( a = 10 \).

Thus, the positive value of \( a \) is \(\boxed{10}\).

문제 10:
Okay, so I have this problem here: the function is f(x) = a cos(bx) + 3, defined on the interval [0, 2π]. It says that the maximum value of f(x) is 13 at x = π/3. I need to find the minimum value of a + b where a and b are natural numbers. Hmm, okay, let's break this down step by step.

First, I remember that the cosine function oscillates between -1 and 1. So, cos(bx) will have a maximum value of 1 and a minimum value of -1. Therefore, the function f(x) = a cos(bx) + 3 will have a maximum value when cos(bx) is 1, which would make f(x) = a*1 + 3 = a + 3. Similarly, the minimum value would be when cos(bx) is -1, so f(x) = a*(-1) + 3 = -a + 3.

But the problem says that the maximum value is 13. So, setting the maximum equal to 13: a + 3 = 13. Solving for a, that gives a = 13 - 3 = 10. So, a is 10. Wait, is that right? Let me double-check. If a is 10, then f(x) = 10 cos(bx) + 3. The maximum of cos(bx) is 1, so 10*1 + 3 = 13, which matches. So, a is indeed 10.

But hold on, the problem also mentions that this maximum occurs at x = π/3. So, I need to make sure that when x is π/3, cos(bx) is 1. Because that's when the maximum happens. So, cos(b*(π/3)) = 1. When does cosine equal 1? At multiples of 2π, right? So, cos(θ) = 1 when θ = 2πk, where k is an integer.

So, setting b*(π/3) = 2πk. Let's solve for b. Dividing both sides by π: (b/3) = 2k. Then, multiplying both sides by 3: b = 6k. So, b must be a multiple of 6. But b is a natural number, so k must be a positive integer (since b has to be at least 1). Therefore, the possible values for b are 6, 12, 18, etc.

But we need to find the minimum value of a + b. Since a is 10, and b is a multiple of 6, the smallest possible b is 6. So, a + b = 10 + 6 = 16. Is that the answer? Wait, let me make sure I didn't miss anything.

Is there a possibility that b could be smaller? Let's think. If b is 6, then the period of the cosine function is 2π / b = 2π / 6 = π/3. So, the function f(x) would have a period of π/3. That means it completes a full cycle every π/3. So, at x = π/3, which is one period length, cos(bx) would indeed be 1 again. So, that makes sense.

But wait, if b is smaller, say b = 3, then the period would be 2π / 3, which is larger than π/3. So, would the maximum still occur at x = π/3? Let's check. If b = 3, then cos(3*(π/3)) = cos(π) = -1. Oh, that's a minimum, not a maximum. So, that's not good. So, if b = 3, the maximum would occur at a different x.

Similarly, if b = 2, then cos(2*(π/3)) = cos(2π/3) = -1/2. Not a maximum. If b = 1, cos(π/3) = 1/2. Still not a maximum. So, in order for cos(b*(π/3)) to be 1, b*(π/3) must be an integer multiple of 2π. So, as I had before, b = 6k.

Therefore, the smallest b is 6, which gives a + b = 16. So, is 16 the minimum value? Let me just confirm.

Wait, another thought: is there a smaller b that could still result in cos(b*(π/3)) = 1? For example, if b*(π/3) = 0, which is 2π*0, but that would mean b = 0, which isn't a natural number. So, the next one is 2π*1, which gives b = 6, as before. So, yeah, 6 is the smallest possible b.

Therefore, a = 10, b = 6, so a + b = 16. So, the minimum value is 16.

But just to make sure, let's plug it back into the function. f(x) = 10 cos(6x) + 3. At x = π/3, cos(6*(π/3)) = cos(2π) = 1. So, f(π/3) = 10*1 + 3 = 13, which is the given maximum. So, that checks out.

Is there any other possible b that is smaller than 6? Let's see. If k = 0, then b = 0, but b must be a natural number, so k must be at least 1. So, b = 6 is indeed the smallest possible.

Therefore, the minimum value of a + b is 16.

**Final Answer**
The minimum value of \(a + b\) is \boxed{16}.
</think>

Given the function \( f(x) = a \cos(bx) + 3 \) defined on the interval \([0, 2\pi]\), we know that the maximum value of \( f(x) \) is 13 at \( x = \frac{\pi}{3} \). We need to find the minimum value of \( a + b \) for natural numbers \( a \) and \( b \).

First, we recognize that the maximum value of \( \cos(bx) \) is 1. Therefore, the maximum value of \( f(x) \) is \( a + 3 \). Given that this maximum is 13, we have:
\[ a + 3 = 13 \]
Solving for \( a \), we get:
\[ a = 10 \]

Next, we need to ensure that the maximum occurs at \( x = \frac{\pi}{3} \). This means:
\[ \cos\left(b \cdot \frac{\pi}{3}\right) = 1 \]
Cosine equals 1 at multiples of \( 2\pi \), so:
\[ b \cdot \frac{\pi}{3} = 2\pi k \]
where \( k \) is an integer. Solving for \( b \), we get:
\[ b = 6k \]

Since \( b \) must be a natural number, the smallest possible value for \( b \) is when \( k = 1 \), giving \( b = 6 \).

Thus, the minimum value of \( a + b \) is:
\[ a + b = 10 + 6 = 16 \]

The minimum value of \( a + b \) is \(\boxed{16}\).

문제 11:
Okay, so I have this problem where a point P starts moving on a number line at time t=0. Its position at any time t is given by the equation x = t³ - (3/2)t² - 6t. I need to find the acceleration of point P at the time when its direction of motion changes after departure. Hmm, okay, let me think about how to approach this.

First, I remember that in kinematics, the position of a point is given by x(t), and the velocity is the first derivative of position with respect to time, v(t) = dx/dt. Acceleration is the second derivative, a(t) = dv/dt = d²x/dt². So, to find the acceleration at a specific time, I need to find the second derivative of the position function and then evaluate it at the time when the direction changes.

But wait, the question is asking for the acceleration at the time when the direction of motion changes. That means I need to find the time when the velocity changes sign, right? Because if the velocity changes from positive to negative or vice versa, the direction of motion changes. So, first, I need to find when the velocity is zero because that's when the direction could change.

So, let me start by finding the velocity function. The position is x(t) = t³ - (3/2)t² - 6t. Taking the derivative with respect to t, the velocity v(t) should be:

v(t) = dx/dt = 3t² - 3t - 6.

Okay, so v(t) = 3t² - 3t - 6. Now, I need to find the times when the velocity is zero because that's when the direction might change. So, I set v(t) equal to zero:

3t² - 3t - 6 = 0.

Let me solve this quadratic equation for t. First, I can factor out a 3:

3(t² - t - 2) = 0.

So, t² - t - 2 = 0. Now, using the quadratic formula, t = [1 ± sqrt(1 + 8)] / 2 = [1 ± 3]/2.

So, t = (1 + 3)/2 = 2, and t = (1 - 3)/2 = -1. But since time t cannot be negative, we discard t = -1. So, the velocity is zero at t = 2 seconds.

Now, I need to check if this is indeed a point where the direction changes. To do that, I can look at the velocity just before and just after t = 2. Let's pick t = 1.5 and t = 2.5.

At t = 1.5: v(1.5) = 3*(1.5)^2 - 3*(1.5) - 6 = 3*(2.25) - 4.5 - 6 = 6.75 - 4.5 - 6 = -3.75. So, negative velocity.

At t = 2.5: v(2.5) = 3*(2.5)^2 - 3*(2.5) - 6 = 3*(6.25) - 7.5 - 6 = 18.75 - 7.5 - 6 = 5.25. Positive velocity.

So, the velocity changes from negative to positive at t = 2, which means the direction of motion changes from left to right (assuming positive direction is to the right). So, t = 2 is indeed the time when the direction changes.

Now, I need to find the acceleration at this time. Acceleration is the second derivative of the position function, so let's compute that.

We already have the first derivative, v(t) = 3t² - 3t - 6. Taking the derivative of that, the acceleration a(t) is:

a(t) = dv/dt = 6t - 3.

So, a(t) = 6t - 3. Now, evaluate this at t = 2:

a(2) = 6*(2) - 3 = 12 - 3 = 9.

So, the acceleration at t = 2 is 9 units per second squared.

Wait, let me double-check my calculations to make sure I didn't make any mistakes.

First, position function: x(t) = t³ - (3/2)t² - 6t. Velocity is derivative: 3t² - 3t - 6. Correct.

Set velocity to zero: 3t² - 3t - 6 = 0. Factor out 3: t² - t - 2 = 0. Solutions t = 2 and t = -1. Discard negative, so t = 2. Correct.

Check velocity before and after t = 2: At t = 1.5, v = -3.75; at t = 2.5, v = 5.25. So, direction changes. Correct.

Acceleration is derivative of velocity: 6t - 3. At t = 2, 6*2 - 3 = 12 - 3 = 9. Correct.

So, all steps seem correct. Therefore, the acceleration at the time when the direction changes is 9.

**Final Answer**
The acceleration of point P when its direction changes is \boxed{9}.
</think>

To find the acceleration of point P at the time when its direction of motion changes, we start with the position function given by \( x(t) = t^3 - \frac{3}{2}t^2 - 6t \).

First, we compute the velocity function by taking the first derivative of the position function:
\[ v(t) = \frac{dx}{dt} = 3t^2 - 3t - 6. \]

Next, we determine the time when the velocity is zero, as this indicates a potential change in direction. Setting the velocity function equal to zero:
\[ 3t^2 - 3t - 6 = 0. \]
Factoring out a 3, we get:
\[ t^2 - t - 2 = 0. \]
Solving this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:
\[ t = \frac{1 \pm \sqrt{1 + 8}}{2} = \frac{1 \pm 3}{2}. \]
This gives the solutions \( t = 2 \) and \( t = -1 \). Since time cannot be negative, we discard \( t = -1 \), leaving \( t = 2 \).

To confirm a direction change, we check the velocity just before and after \( t = 2 \). At \( t = 1.5 \), the velocity is negative, and at \( t = 2.5 \), the velocity is positive, indicating a direction change at \( t = 2 \).

Next, we find the acceleration function by taking the derivative of the velocity function:
\[ a(t) = \frac{dv}{dt} = 6t - 3. \]

Evaluating the acceleration at \( t = 2 \):
\[ a(2) = 6(2) - 3 = 12 - 3 = 9. \]

Thus, the acceleration of point P when its direction changes is \(\boxed{9}\).

So, the sum becomes Sum_{k=1}^n [a_k / (2 + k*d)] = (1/2) n^2.

**Final Answer**
The value of $\sum_{k=1}^5 a_k$ is \boxed{120}.
</think>

Given the sequence $\{a_n\}$ with $a_1 = 2$ and the arithmetic sequence $\{b_n\}$ with $b_1 = 2$, we know that for all natural numbers $n$,

$$ \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{a_k}{b_{k+1}} = \frac{1}{2} n^2. $$

We need to find the value of $\sum_{k=1}^5 a_k$.

First, we recognize that $\{b_n\}$ is an arithmetic sequence with the first term $b_1 = 2$ and common difference $d$. Thus, $b_n = 2 + (n-1)d$. For $b_{k+1}$, this becomes $2 + kd$.

Given the condition, we express the sum as:

$$ \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{a_k}{2 + kd} = \frac{1}{2} n^2. $$

By considering the difference between consecutive sums, we find:

$$ \frac{a_n}{2 + nd} = n - \frac{1}{2}. $$

Thus,

$$ a_n = (n - \frac{1}{2})(2 + nd). $$

Using the initial condition for $n=1$, we find $d = 2$. Therefore, $b_n = 2n$ and $b_{k+1} = 2(k+1)$. Substituting $d = 2$ into the expression for $a_n$, we get:

$$ a_n = (n - \frac{1}{2})(2 + 2n) = 2(n - \frac{1}{2})(n + 1). $$

Expanding this, we find:

$$ a_n = 2n^2 + n - 1. $$

We then compute the first five terms of $\{a_n\}$:

- $a_1 = 2(1)^2 + 1 - 1 = 2$
- $a_2 = 2(2)^2 + 2 - 1 = 9$
- $a_3 = 2(3)^2 + 3 - 1 = 20$
- $a_4 = 2(4)^2 + 4 - 1 = 35$
- $a_5 = 2(5)^2 + 5 - 1 = 54$

Summing these terms:

$$ \sum_{k=1}^5 a_k = 2 + 9 + 20 + 35 + 54 = 120. $$

Thus, the value of $\sum_{k=1}^5 a_k$ is \boxed{120}.

First, let's compute A:

A = ∫₀^{Q_x} (x³ -7x +6 -4x) dx = ∫₀^{Q_x} (x³ -11x +6) dx

Similarly, B = ∫_{Q_x}^3 (4x - (x³ -7x +6)) dx = ∫_{Q_x}^3 (-x³ +11x -6) dx

So, let's compute these integrals.

First, compute A:

A = ∫ (x³ -11x +6) dx from 0 to Q_x

The antiderivative is:

(1/4)x⁴ - (11/2)x² +6x evaluated from 0 to Q_x

Similarly, B:

B = ∫ (-x³ +11x -6) dx from Q_x to 3

Antiderivative is:

- (1/4)x⁴ + (11/2)x² -6x evaluated from Q_x to 3

So, let's compute A and B.

But before that, let's note that Q_x is a root of x³ -11x +6=0, because f(x)=4x at Q_x, so x³ -11x +6=0.

Therefore, Q_x³ =11Q_x -6

This might help in simplifying the expressions.

Let me compute A:

A = [ (1/4)Q_x⁴ - (11/2)Q_x² +6Q_x ] - [0 -0 +0] = (1/4)Q_x⁴ - (11/2)Q_x² +6Q_x

But since Q_x³ =11Q_x -6, we can express higher powers in terms of lower powers.

Compute Q_x⁴:

Q_x⁴ = Q_x * Q_x³ = Q_x*(11Q_x -6) =11Q_x² -6Q_x

So, substitute back into A:

A = (1/4)(11Q_x² -6Q_x) - (11/2)Q_x² +6Q_x

Simplify:

= (11/4)Q_x² - (6/4)Q_x - (11/2)Q_x² +6Q_x

Convert to common denominators:

= (11/4)Q_x² - (3/2)Q_x - (22/4)Q_x² + (24/4)Q_x

Combine like terms:

(11/4 -22/4)Q_x² + (-3/2 +24/4)Q_x

= (-11/4)Q_x² + ( -3/2 +6 )Q_x

Convert 6 to 12/2:

= (-11/4)Q_x² + ( -3/2 +12/2 )Q_x

= (-11/4)Q_x² + (9/2)Q_x

Now, factor:

= (-11/4)Q_x² + (9/2)Q_x

Similarly, let's compute B:

B = [ - (1/4)(3)⁴ + (11/2)(3)² -6*(3) ] - [ - (1/4)Q_x⁴ + (11/2)Q_x² -6Q_x ]

Compute each part:

First, evaluate at x=3:

- (1/4)(81) + (11/2)(9) -18 = -81/4 + 99/2 -18

Convert to common denominator, which is 4:

= -81/4 + 198/4 -72/4 = ( -81 +198 -72 ) /4 = (45)/4

Now, evaluate at x=Q_x:

- (1/4)Q_x⁴ + (11/2)Q_x² -6Q_x

Again, we can use Q_x³=11Q_x -6, so Q_x⁴=11Q_x² -6Q_x

Thus:

= - (1/4)(11Q_x² -6Q_x) + (11/2)Q_x² -6Q_x

= - (11/4)Q_x² + (6/4)Q_x + (11/2)Q_x² -6Q_x

Simplify:

= (-11/4 +22/4)Q_x² + (6/4 -24/4)Q_x

= (11/4)Q_x² - (18/4)Q_x

= (11/4)Q_x² - (9/2)Q_x

So, putting it all together:

B = [45/4] - [ (11/4)Q_x² - (9/2)Q_x ] = 45/4 -11/4 Q_x² +9/2 Q_x

Now, let's write A and B:

A = (-11/4)Q_x² + (9/2)Q_x

B = 45/4 -11/4 Q_x² +9/2 Q_x

So, B - A = [45/4 -11/4 Q_x² +9/2 Q_x] - [ -11/4 Q_x² +9/2 Q_x ]

Simplify:

= 45/4 -11/4 Q_x² +9/2 Q_x +11/4 Q_x² -9/2 Q_x

The terms with Q_x² and Q_x cancel out:

= 45/4

So, B - A = 45/4

Convert to mixed number if needed, but since the question asks for the value, 45/4 is acceptable.

But let me double-check the calculations to make sure I didn't make a mistake.

We had:

A = (-11/4)Q_x² + (9/2)Q_x

B = 45/4 -11/4 Q_x² +9/2 Q_x

So, B - A = 45/4 -11/4 Q_x² +9/2 Q_x - (-11/4 Q_x² +9/2 Q_x )

= 45/4 -11/4 Q_x² +9/2 Q_x +11/4 Q_x² -9/2 Q_x

Yes, the Q_x terms cancel, leaving 45/4.

So, B - A = 45/4, which is 11.25.

But let me think again: the integral from 0 to Q_x of (f(x)-4x)dx is A, and from Q_x to3 of (4x -f(x))dx is B.

Then, B - A = [∫_{Q_x}^3 (4x -f(x))dx] - [∫₀^{Q_x} (f(x)-4x)dx]

= ∫_{Q_x}^3 (4x -f(x))dx + ∫₀^{Q_x} (4x -f(x))dx

= ∫₀^3 (4x -f(x))dx

Because we're combining the two integrals over the entire interval [0,3].

So, B - A = ∫₀^3 (4x -f(x))dx

Which is the total area between the curve and the line from 0 to3.

But wait, that's a simpler way to compute it.

So, instead of computing A and B separately, B - A is just the integral from 0 to3 of (4x -f(x))dx.

Let me compute that:

∫₀^3 (4x - (x³ -7x +6)) dx = ∫₀^3 (4x -x³ +7x -6) dx = ∫₀^3 (-x³ +11x -6) dx

Antiderivative:

- (1/4)x⁴ + (11/2)x² -6x evaluated from 0 to3

At x=3:

- (1/4)(81) + (11/2)(9) -18 = -81/4 + 99/2 -18

Convert to quarters:

-81/4 + 198/4 -72/4 = (-81 +198 -72)/4 = (45)/4

At x=0, all terms are 0.

So, the integral is 45/4.

Therefore, B - A =45/4.

So, the value is 45/4, which is 11.25.

But the problem might expect an exact value, which is 45/4.

So, the answer is 45/4.

I think that's correct. Let me just recap:

By realizing that B - A is the integral from 0 to3 of (4x -f(x))dx, which simplifies the calculation, avoiding dealing with Q_x explicitly. The integral evaluates to 45/4.

**Final Answer**
\boxed{\dfrac{45}{4}}

Given a cubic function \( f(x) \) with leading coefficient 1, we know that \( f(1) = f(2) = 0 \) and \( f'(0) = -7 \). The function is determined to be \( f(x) = x^3 - 7x + 6 \).

The point \( P \) is \( (3, f(3)) \), which is \( (3, 12) \). The line segment \( OP \) has the equation \( y = 4x \). The intersection of the curve \( y = f(x) \) and the line \( y = 4x \) other than \( P \) is point \( Q \). Solving \( f(x) = 4x \), we find the roots \( x = 3 \) and \( x = \frac{-3 + \sqrt{17}}{2} \). Thus, \( Q \) is \( \left( \frac{\sqrt{17} - 3}{2}, 2\sqrt{17} - 6 \right) \).

To find the areas \( A \) and \( B \):

- \( A \) is the area enclosed by the curve \( y = f(x) \), the y-axis, and the segment \( OQ \).
- \( B \) is the area enclosed by the curve \( y = f(x) \) and the segment \( PQ \).

We compute the integral of the difference between the line \( y = 4x \) and the curve \( y = f(x) \) from 0 to 3. This integral gives the value of \( B - A \).

The integral is:
\[
\int_0^3 (4x - (x^3 - 7x + 6)) \, dx = \int_0^3 (-x^3 + 11x - 6) \, dx
\]

Evaluating this integral, we find:
\[
\left[ -\frac{1}{4}x^4 + \frac{11}{2}x^2 - 6x \right]_0^3 = \left( -\frac{81}{4} + \frac{99}{2} - 18 \right) - \left( 0 \right) = \frac{45}{4}
\]

Thus, the value of \( B - A \) is:
\[
\boxed{\dfrac{45}{4}}
\]

So, perhaps my initial assumption was wrong. The maximum distance is not 15/2 + 3√3, but rather 15/2 + (3√3)/2.

Wait, so perhaps the maximum distance is achieved not at (0, -3√3), but somewhere else on the circle.

Wait, maybe I need to parametrize point P on circle O and find the maximum distance to BC.

Let me parametrize circle O as (3√3 cos θ, 3√3 sin θ). Then, the distance from P to line BC is |√3 x + y -15| / 2.

So, substituting x = 3√3 cos θ, y = 3√3 sin θ:

Distance = |√3*(3√3 cos θ) + 3√3 sin θ -15| / 2 = | (9 cos θ) + 3√3 sin θ -15 | / 2.

So, to maximize this distance, we need to maximize |9 cos θ + 3√3 sin θ -15|.

Since the expression inside the absolute value is 9 cos θ + 3√3 sin θ -15. The maximum of |expression| occurs either at the maximum or minimum of the expression.

So, let me consider the expression 9 cos θ + 3√3 sin θ -15.

Let me denote this as E(θ) = 9 cos θ + 3√3 sin θ -15.

To find the maximum of |E(θ)|, we can find the maximum and minimum of E(θ).

First, note that 9 cos θ + 3√3 sin θ can be written as R cos(θ - φ), where R = sqrt(9^2 + (3√3)^2) = sqrt(81 + 27) = sqrt(108) = 6√3.

So, E(θ) = 6√3 cos(θ - φ) -15, where φ is such that cos φ = 9 / (6√3) = (9)/(6√3) = (3)/(2√3) = √3/2, and sin φ = (3√3)/ (6√3) = 1/2.

So, φ is 30 degrees or π/6 radians.

Therefore, E(θ) = 6√3 cos(θ - π/6) -15.

So, the maximum value of E(θ) is when cos(θ - π/6) = 1, so E_max = 6√3*1 -15 = 6√3 -15.

The minimum value is when cos(θ - π/6) = -1, so E_min = -6√3 -15.

Therefore, |E(θ)| can achieve maximum at either E_max or |E_min|.

Compute |E_max| = |6√3 -15| ≈ |10.392 -15| ≈ 4.608.

Compute |E_min| = | -6√3 -15 | = 6√3 +15 ≈ 10.392 +15 ≈ 25.392.

So, the maximum of |E(θ)| is 6√3 +15.

Therefore, the maximum distance is (6√3 +15)/2.

So, h_max = (6√3 +15)/2.

Therefore, the maximum area is (1/2)*|BC|*h_max = (1/2)*(8√3)*[(6√3 +15)/2] = (4√3)*(6√3 +15)/2 = (4√3/2)*(6√3 +15) = 2√3*(6√3 +15).

Compute this:

2√3*6√3 = 12*3 = 36

2√3*15 = 30√3

So, total area is 36 + 30√3.

So, the maximum area of triangle PBC is 36 + 30√3.

The maximum area of triangle PBC is \boxed{36 + 30\sqrt{3}}.

Given triangle \(ABC\) with point \(D\) on segment \(AB\) such that \(\overline{AD}:\overline{DB} = 3:2\). Circle \(O\) is centered at \(A\) and passes through \(D\), intersecting segment \(AC\) at \(E\). The ratio \(\sin A : \sin C = 8:5\) and the ratio of the areas of triangles \(ADE\) and \(ABC\) is \(9:35\). The circumradius of triangle \(ABC\) is 7. We need to find the maximum area of triangle \(PBC\) for a point \(P\) on circle \(O\).

1. **Using the Law of Sines:**
   - Given \(\sin A : \sin C = 8:5\), we find the sides of triangle \(ABC\) using the circumradius \(R = 7\).
   - Sides \(a = BC\), \(b = AC\), and \(c = AB\) are in the ratio \(8:7:5\).

2. **Area Ratio:**
   - The area ratio of triangles \(ADE\) and \(ABC\) is \(9:35\).
   - Using the area formula, we find the sides of triangle \(ABC\) as \(AB = 5\sqrt{3}\), \(AC = 7\sqrt{3}\), and \(BC = 8\sqrt{3}\).

3. **Coordinates of Points:**
   - Placing \(A\) at the origin \((0,0)\), \(B\) at \((5\sqrt{3}, 0)\), and finding coordinates of \(C\) as \((\sqrt{3}, 12)\).

4. **Circle \(O\) and Point \(E\):**
   - Circle \(O\) centered at \(A\) with radius \(3\sqrt{3}\).
   - Point \(E\) on \(AC\) such that \(AE = AD = 3\sqrt{3}\).

5. **Maximizing the Area of Triangle \(PBC\):**
   - The maximum area occurs when the height from \(P\) to \(BC\) is maximized.
   - The distance from \(A\) to line \(BC\) is \(7.5\), and the radius of circle \(O\) is \(3\sqrt{3}\).
   - The maximum distance from \(P\) to \(BC\) is \(\frac{15}{2} + 3\sqrt{3}\).

6. **Calculating the Maximum Area:**
   - The maximum area of triangle \(PBC\) is \(\frac{1}{2} \times |BC| \times \text{maximum height}\).
   - This results in the area \(\frac{1}{2} \times 8\sqrt{3} \times \left(\frac{15}{2} + 3\sqrt{3}\right)\).

7. **Final Calculation:**
   - Simplifying, the maximum area is \(36 + 30\sqrt{3}\).

Thus, the maximum area of triangle \(PBC\) is \(\boxed{36 + 30\sqrt{3}}\).

The problem states two main conditions:

(a) \( g(x) \) is differentiable for all real numbers. That means at \( x = 0 \), the left-hand derivative and the right-hand derivative must be equal. So, I need to ensure that the derivatives from both sides match at that point.

(b) The equation \( g'(x) \times g'(x - 4) = 0 \) has 4 distinct real roots. This means that the product of the derivatives at points \( x \) and \( x - 4 \) equals zero. So, either \( g'(x) = 0 \) or \( g'(x - 4) = 0 \). This equation having four distinct real roots implies that there are four different x-values where either the derivative at x is zero or the derivative at x - 4 is zero.

My goal is to find \( g(-2) + g(2) \). Let's break this down.

First, let's handle condition (a): differentiability at \( x = 0 \).

For \( x \leq 0 \), \( g(x) = x^3 + a x^2 + 15x + 7 \). So, the derivative \( g'(x) \) for \( x \leq 0 \) is \( 3x^2 + 2a x + 15 \).

For \( x > 0 \), \( g(x) = f(x) \), which is quadratic. Let's denote \( f(x) = px^2 + qx + r \). Since it's quadratic with a negative leading coefficient, \( p < 0 \).

The derivative \( f'(x) = 2px + q \).

At \( x = 0 \), the left-hand derivative is \( g'(0^-) = 3(0)^2 + 2a(0) + 15 = 15 \).

The right-hand derivative is \( g'(0^+) = 2p(0) + q = q \).

\( 15 = q \).

Given the piecewise function \( g(x) \) defined as:
\[ g(x) = \begin{cases} 
x^3 + a x^2 + 15x + 7 & \text{if } x \leq 0 \\
f(x) & \text{if } x > 0 
\end{cases} \]
where \( f(x) \) is a quadratic function with a negative leading coefficient, we need to find the value of \( g(-2) + g(2) \).

### Key Steps:

1. **Differentiability at \( x = 0 \)**:
   - The left-hand derivative at \( x = 0 \) is \( 15 \).
   - The right-hand derivative at \( x = 0 \) must also be \( 15 \), leading to \( q = 15 \).

2. **Critical Points and Differentiability**:
   - For \( x \leq 0 \), the derivative is \( 3x^2 + 2ax + 15 \).
   - For \( x > 0 \), the derivative is \( 2px + 15 \).
   - The equation \( g'(x) \times g'(x - 4) = 0 \) must have 4 distinct real roots.

3. **Finding the Value of \( a \)**:
   - The quadratic \( 3x^2 + 2ax + 15 = 0 \) has roots \( c_1 \) and \( c_2 \).
   - The quadratic \( f(x) \) has a critical point at \( x = -15/(2p) \).
   - By analyzing the roots and ensuring the equation \( g'(x) \times g'(x - 4) = 0 \) has four distinct real roots, we find \( a = 7 \).

4. **Calculating \( g(-2) \) and \( g(2) \)**:
   - For \( x = -2 \), \( g(-2) = (-2)^3 + 7(-2)^2 + 15(-2) + 7 = -3 \).
   - For \( x = 2 \), \( g(2) = f(2) = -7.5(2)^2 + 15(2) + 7 = 7 \).

5. **Summing the Values**:
   - \( g(-2) + g(2) = -3 + 7 = 4 \).
