**Final Answer**
\boxed{25}
</think>

Given the geometric sequence \(\{a_n\}\), we have the following series sums:

1. \(\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} (|a_n| + a_n) = \frac{40}{3}\)
2. \(\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} (|a_n| - a_n) = \frac{20}{3}\)

From these, we determine that the sum of the positive terms \(S_{\text{plus}} = \frac{20}{3}\) and the sum of the absolute values of the negative terms \(S_{\text{minus}} = \frac{10}{3}\). This implies the geometric sequence has a common ratio \(r = -\frac{1}{2}\) and the first term \(a = 5\). Thus, the sequence is given by \(a_n = 5 \left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{n-1}\).

Next, we need to evaluate the limit:

\[
\lim_{n \rightarrow \infty} \sum_{k=1}^{2n} \left( (-1)^{\frac{k(k+1)}{2}} \times a_{m+k} \right)
\]

We find that the exponent \(\frac{k(k+1)}{2}\) results in a periodic sign pattern \([-1, -1, 1, 1]\). This allows us to express the sum as a geometric series. After simplification, we find that the limit is \(\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{m-1}\).

We need this limit to be greater than \(\frac{1}{700}\). This results in the inequality:

\[
\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{m-1} > \frac{1}{700}
\]

Solving this, we find that \(m\) must be an odd natural number such that \(m-1 < \log_{2}(700)\). This gives us \(m = 1, 3, 5, 7, 9\). Summing these values, we get:

\[
1 + 3 + 5 + 7 + 9 = 25
\]

Thus, the sum of all such \(m\) is:

\[
\boxed{25}
\]

문제 30:
Okay, so I have this problem here about a function f(x) = sin(ax + b + sinx). The constants a and b are given with 1 ≤ a ≤ 2. There are two conditions: first, f(0) = 0 and f(2π) = 2πa + b. Second, the smallest positive t such that f’(0) = f’(t) is 4π. Then, I need to find the set A of all α in (0, 4π) where f has a local maximum. Then, n is the number of elements in A, α₁ is the smallest one, and then compute nα₁ - ab, which equals q/p π, and find p + q.

Alright, let me break this down step by step.

First, let's write down the function: f(x) = sin(ax + b + sinx). So, it's a sine function with a phase shift and a modified argument because of the sinx inside.

Given that f(0) = 0. Let's compute f(0):

f(0) = sin(a*0 + b + sin0) = sin(b + 0) = sin(b) = 0.

So, sin(b) = 0. That implies that b is an integer multiple of π. So, b = kπ for some integer k.

Next, f(2π) = sin(a*2π + b + sin(2π)) = sin(2πa + b + 0) = sin(2πa + b). According to the problem, this is equal to 2πa + b.

So, sin(2πa + b) = 2πa + b.

Hmm, that's interesting. So, sin(θ) = θ, where θ = 2πa + b.

But sin(θ) = θ only when θ = 0, because sin(θ) is bounded between -1 and 1, and θ is a real number. So, unless θ is 0, sin(θ) can't equal θ. So, 2πa + b must be 0.

But wait, 2πa is positive because a is between 1 and 2, so 2πa is between 2π and 4π. And b is kπ. So, 2πa + b = 0 would require b = -2πa. But since a is at least 1, b would be at most -2π. But b is an integer multiple of π, so that's possible.

Wait, but earlier, from f(0) = 0, we have b = kπ. So, if 2πa + b = 0, then b = -2πa. So, combining both, b must be equal to both kπ and -2πa. So, -2πa = kπ, which implies that k = -2a.

But k is an integer, and a is between 1 and 2. So, -2a is between -4 and -2. So, k must be -4, -3, or -2. But a is between 1 and 2, so let's see:

If k = -2, then -2a = -2 => a = 1.

If k = -3, then -2a = -3 => a = 3/2.

If k = -4, then -2a = -4 => a = 2.

So, possible a values are 1, 3/2, and 2, with corresponding b values:

For a=1, b = -2π*1 = -2π.

For a=3/2, b = -2π*(3/2) = -3π.

For a=2, b = -2π*2 = -4π.

So, possible pairs (a, b) are (1, -2π), (3/2, -3π), (2, -4π).

But wait, the problem says 1 ≤ a ≤ 2, so these are all valid.

But we also have another condition: the smallest positive t such that f’(0) = f’(t) is 4π.

So, let's compute f’(x). The derivative of f(x) is:

f’(x) = cos(ax + b + sinx) * (a + cosx).

So, f’(0) = cos(a*0 + b + sin0) * (a + cos0) = cos(b) * (a + 1).

But from earlier, b = kπ, so cos(b) = cos(kπ) = (-1)^k.

So, f’(0) = (-1)^k * (a + 1).

We need to find the smallest positive t such that f’(t) = f’(0). So, f’(t) = cos(a t + b + sin t) * (a + cos t) = (-1)^k * (a + 1).

And the smallest such t is 4π.

So, let's analyze this.

First, let's note that for each possible a and b, we can compute f’(0) and then find t where f’(t) equals that.

But since a and b are dependent on k, which is -2a, as above, let's consider each case.

Case 1: a = 1, b = -2π.

Then, f’(0) = (-1)^k * (1 + 1). Since k = -2a = -2, so k = -2. So, (-1)^{-2} = 1. So, f’(0) = 1 * 2 = 2.

So, f’(t) = 2. So, we have:

cos(t + (-2π) + sin t) * (1 + cos t) = 2.

But cos(t - 2π + sin t) = cos(t + sin t - 2π) = cos(t + sin t), since cosine is 2π periodic.

So, equation becomes:

cos(t + sin t) * (1 + cos t) = 2.

But the maximum value of cos(t + sin t) is 1, and the maximum of (1 + cos t) is 2. So, the product can be at most 2. So, equality occurs when both factors are 1 and 2, respectively, or 2 and 1. But 1 + cos t can be 2 only when cos t = 1, which is at t = 2πn. Similarly, cos(t + sin t) = 1 when t + sin t = 2πn.

So, let's set t = 2πn, then t + sin t = 2πn + sin(2πn) = 2πn + 0 = 2πn. So, cos(2πn) = 1. So, yes, at t = 2πn, both factors are 1 and 2, so the product is 2.

So, the solutions are t = 2πn, n integer. So, the smallest positive t is 2π. But the problem says the smallest positive t is 4π. So, in this case, it's 2π, which contradicts the given condition. So, a=1 is invalid.

Case 2: a = 3/2, b = -3π.

Then, f’(0) = (-1)^k * (3/2 + 1). Here, k = -2a = -3, so k = -3. So, (-1)^{-3} = -1. So, f’(0) = (-1) * (5/2) = -5/2.

So, f’(t) = -5/2.

So, equation is:

cos((3/2)t - 3π + sin t) * (3/2 + cos t) = -5/2.

Hmm, this seems more complicated. Let's see.

First, let's note that the maximum of cos(...) is 1, and the minimum is -1. The term (3/2 + cos t) has a maximum of 3/2 + 1 = 5/2 and a minimum of 3/2 - 1 = 1/2.

So, the product of cos(...) * (3/2 + cos t) can range between (-1)*(5/2) = -5/2 and (1)*(5/2) = 5/2.

So, -5/2 is attainable when cos(...) = -1 and (3/2 + cos t) = 5/2. So, when does (3/2 + cos t) = 5/2? That's when cos t = 1, so t = 2πn.

At t = 2πn, cos((3/2)t - 3π + sin t) = cos(3πn - 3π + 0) = cos(3π(n - 1)).

So, cos(3π(n - 1)) = (-1)^{3(n - 1)} = (-1)^{n - 1} because 3 is odd.

So, if n is odd, cos(3π(n - 1)) = cos(3π even) = cos(6πk) = 1.

Wait, no, wait. Let me compute it correctly.

Wait, 3π(n - 1). If n is an integer, then 3π(n - 1) is a multiple of 3π. So, cos(3π(n - 1)) = cos(3πk) where k = n - 1.

cos(3πk) = (-1)^{3k} = (-1)^k, since 3k mod 2 is same as k mod 2.

So, cos(3π(n - 1)) = (-1)^{n - 1}.

So, at t = 2πn, cos((3/2)t - 3π + sin t) = (-1)^{n - 1}.

So, the product is (-1)^{n - 1} * (5/2). So, to get -5/2, we need (-1)^{n - 1} = -1, so n - 1 must be odd, so n must be even.

So, the smallest positive t is t = 2π*2 = 4π.

So, that's exactly the condition given in the problem. So, this case satisfies the condition.

Case 3: a = 2, b = -4π.

Then, f’(0) = (-1)^k * (2 + 1) = (-1)^k * 3. Here, k = -2a = -4, so k = -4. So, (-1)^{-4} = 1. So, f’(0) = 3.

So, f’(t) = 3.

Equation: cos(2t - 4π + sin t) * (2 + cos t) = 3.

Again, cos(2t - 4π + sin t) = cos(2t + sin t - 4π) = cos(2t + sin t), since cosine is 2π periodic.

So, equation becomes:

cos(2t + sin t) * (2 + cos t) = 3.

The maximum of cos(2t + sin t) is 1, and the maximum of (2 + cos t) is 3. So, the product can be at most 3. So, equality occurs when both factors are 1 and 3, respectively.

So, 2 + cos t = 3 implies cos t = 1, so t = 2πn.

At t = 2πn, cos(2t + sin t) = cos(4πn + 0) = cos(4πn) = 1.

So, the product is 1 * 3 = 3. So, the solutions are t = 2πn, n integer. So, the smallest positive t is 2π, which again contradicts the given condition of 4π. So, a=2 is invalid.

Therefore, only a=3/2 and b=-3π satisfy both conditions.

So, now, we have a=3/2, b=-3π.

So, f(x) = sin((3/2)x - 3π + sinx).

We can simplify this a bit. Let's write it as:

f(x) = sin((3/2)x + sinx - 3π).

But sin(θ - 3π) = sin(θ - π - 2π) = sin(θ - π) because sin is 2π periodic. And sin(θ - π) = -sin(θ). So, sin(θ - 3π) = -sinθ.

Wait, let me verify:

sin(θ - 3π) = sinθ cos3π - cosθ sin3π = sinθ*(-1) - cosθ*(0) = -sinθ.

Yes, correct. So, sin(θ - 3π) = -sinθ.

So, f(x) = sin((3/2)x + sinx - 3π) = -sin((3/2)x + sinx).

So, f(x) = -sin((3/2)x + sinx).

Alternatively, f(x) = sin((3/2)x + sinx - 3π) = sin((3/2)x + sinx - π - 2π) = sin((3/2)x + sinx - π), since sin is 2π periodic.

And sin(θ - π) = -sinθ, so f(x) = -sin((3/2)x + sinx). So, same result.

So, f(x) = -sin((3/2)x + sinx).

Alright, so now, we need to find the set A of all α in (0, 4π) where f has a local maximum.

So, to find local maxima, we need to find critical points where the derivative is zero and the second derivative is negative.

But first, let's compute the derivative f’(x):

f’(x) = -cos((3/2)x + sinx) * (3/2 + cosx).

So, critical points occur when f’(x) = 0.

So, -cos((3/2)x + sinx) * (3/2 + cosx) = 0.

This product is zero when either cos((3/2)x + sinx) = 0 or (3/2 + cosx) = 0.

But 3/2 + cosx is always positive because cosx ≥ -1, so 3/2 + cosx ≥ 3/2 - 1 = 1/2 > 0. So, 3/2 + cosx is never zero. Therefore, critical points occur only when cos((3/2)x + sinx) = 0.

So, cos(θ) = 0 when θ = π/2 + kπ, k integer.

Therefore, (3/2)x + sinx = π/2 + kπ.

So, we need to solve for x in (0, 4π):

(3/2)x + sinx = π/2 + kπ.

So, for each integer k, we can attempt to solve this equation.

Our goal is to find all solutions x in (0, 4π). So, let's find the range of (3/2)x + sinx over x ∈ (0, 4π).

Compute the minimum and maximum of (3/2)x + sinx on (0, 4π).

The derivative of (3/2)x + sinx is 3/2 + cosx.

Set derivative to zero: 3/2 + cosx = 0 => cosx = -3/2, which is impossible because cosx is between -1 and 1. So, the function (3/2)x + sinx is strictly increasing on (0, 4π) because its derivative is always positive (3/2 + cosx ≥ 3/2 - 1 = 1/2 > 0). So, it's a strictly increasing function.

Therefore, (3/2)x + sinx is a bijection from (0, 4π) to its range.

Compute the range:

At x=0: (3/2)*0 + sin0 = 0.

At x=4π: (3/2)*4π + sin(4π) = 6π + 0 = 6π.

So, (3/2)x + sinx increases from 0 to 6π as x goes from 0 to 4π.

Therefore, the equation (3/2)x + sinx = π/2 + kπ has exactly one solution for each k such that π/2 + kπ is in (0, 6π).

So, let's find all integers k such that 0 < π/2 + kπ < 6π.

Solve for k:

π/2 + kπ > 0 => k ≥ 0 (since k is integer, k ≥ 0).

π/2 + kπ < 6π => kπ < 6π - π/2 => kπ < (11/2)π => k < 11/2 => k ≤ 5.

So, k can be 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5.

Therefore, there are 6 solutions in (0, 4π). So, n = 6.

Wait, but hold on. Let me check.

Wait, for k=0: π/2 ≈ 1.57 < 6π, so x exists.

For k=5: π/2 + 5π = 11π/2 ≈ 17.27 < 6π ≈ 18.84, so yes.

k=6: π/2 + 6π = 13π/2 ≈ 20.42 > 6π, so no.

So, k=0,1,2,3,4,5: 6 values.

Therefore, 6 solutions, so n=6.

But wait, the problem says "the set of all values of α in the open interval (0, 4π)" where f has a local maximum. So, each critical point is a solution to (3/2)x + sinx = π/2 + kπ, and since the function is strictly increasing, each k gives exactly one x in (0, 4π). So, 6 critical points.

But we need to determine which of these correspond to local maxima.

So, to determine whether each critical point is a maximum or minimum, we can use the second derivative test.

Compute f''(x):

f’(x) = -cos((3/2)x + sinx) * (3/2 + cosx).

So, f''(x) is the derivative of f’(x):

First, let me denote u = (3/2)x + sinx, so f’(x) = -cos(u) * (3/2 + cosx).

Then, f''(x) = derivative of [-cos(u)*(3/2 + cosx)].

Use product rule:

= [sin(u) * u'] * (3/2 + cosx) + [-cos(u)] * (-sinx).

So, f''(x) = sin(u) * u' * (3/2 + cosx) + cos(u) * sinx.

Where u' = 3/2 + cosx.

So, f''(x) = sin(u)*(3/2 + cosx)^2 + cos(u)*sinx.

At critical points, u = π/2 + kπ, so sin(u) = sin(π/2 + kπ) = (-1)^k, and cos(u) = cos(π/2 + kπ) = 0.

Therefore, at critical points, f''(x) = (-1)^k * (3/2 + cosx)^2 + 0.

So, f''(x) = (-1)^k * (3/2 + cosx)^2.

Since (3/2 + cosx)^2 is always positive, the sign of f''(x) is determined by (-1)^k.

Therefore, if k is even, f''(x) is positive, which means the function is concave upwards, so it's a local minimum.

If k is odd, f''(x) is negative, so concave downwards, which is a local maximum.

Therefore, the critical points where k is odd correspond to local maxima.

So, among the 6 critical points (k=0 to 5), the ones with k odd (k=1,3,5) are local maxima.

Therefore, the number of local maxima is 3, so n=3.

Wait, hold on. Wait, k=0: even, minimum.

k=1: odd, maximum.

k=2: even, minimum.

k=3: odd, maximum.

k=4: even, minimum.

k=5: odd, maximum.

So, yes, 3 maxima.

Therefore, set A has 3 elements, so n=3.

Now, we need to find α₁, the smallest α in A, which corresponds to the smallest x in (0,4π) where f has a local maximum, which is the solution for k=1.

So, we need to solve (3/2)x + sinx = π/2 + π = 3π/2.

So, equation: (3/2)x + sinx = 3π/2.

We need to solve for x in (0,4π). Since the function (3/2)x + sinx is strictly increasing, there is exactly one solution.

Let me denote g(x) = (3/2)x + sinx.

We need to find x such that g(x) = 3π/2.

We can attempt to approximate this solution or find an exact expression if possible.

But since it's a transcendental equation, we might not have an exact solution, but perhaps we can express it in terms of known constants or find a clever substitution.

Alternatively, maybe we can relate it to the given conditions.

Wait, let's think about the function g(x) = (3/2)x + sinx.

We know that g(0) = 0, and g(4π) = 6π.

We need to find x such that g(x) = 3π/2.

Let me try plugging in x=π.

g(π) = (3/2)π + sinπ = (3π/2) + 0 = 3π/2.

Oh! So, x=π is the solution.

Wow, that's neat. So, α₁ = π.

So, the smallest local maximum is at x=π.

Therefore, α₁ = π.

So, now, n=3, α₁=π.

Compute nα₁ - ab.

Given that a=3/2, b=-3π.

So, ab = (3/2)*(-3π) = -9π/2.

So, nα₁ - ab = 3*π - (-9π/2) = 3π + 9π/2 = (6π + 9π)/2 = 15π/2.

So, 15π/2 is equal to q/p π, so q=15, p=2. They are coprime.

Therefore, p + q = 2 + 15 = 17.

So, the answer is 17.

**Final Answer**
\boxed{17}
</think>

Given the function \( f(x) = \sin(ax + b + \sin x) \) with constants \( a \) and \( b \) where \( 1 \leq a \leq 2 \), we need to determine the value of \( p + q \) where \( n\alpha_1 - ab = \frac{q}{p}\pi \) and \( p \) and \( q \) are coprime natural numbers.

1. **Determine \( b \) from \( f(0) = 0 \)**:
   - \( f(0) = \sin(b) = 0 \) implies \( b = k\pi \) for some integer \( k \).

2. **Determine \( a \) and \( b \) from \( f(2\pi) = 2\pi a + b \)**:
   - \( f(2\pi) = \sin(2\pi a + b) = 2\pi a + b \).
   - This implies \( 2\pi a + b = 0 \) because \( \sin(\theta) = \theta \) only when \( \theta = 0 \).
   - Thus, \( b = -2\pi a \).

3. **Check possible values of \( a \) and \( b \)**:
   - Possible pairs are \( (1, -2\pi) \), \( (3/2, -3\pi) \), and \( (2, -4\pi) \).

4. **Verify the smallest positive \( t \) such that \( f'(0) = f'(t) \) is \( 4\pi \)**:
   - For \( a = 3/2 \) and \( b = -3\pi \), the smallest positive \( t \) is \( 4\pi \).

5. **Find local maxima of \( f(x) \)**:
   - The function \( f(x) = -\sin((3/2)x + \sin x) \).
   - Critical points occur when \( \cos((3/2)x + \sin x) = 0 \), leading to \( (3/2)x + \sin x = \pi/2 + k\pi \).
   - Solutions for \( x \) in \( (0, 4\pi) \) are found for \( k = 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 \).
   - Local maxima occur when \( k \) is odd, giving 3 solutions.

6. **Determine the smallest local maximum \( \alpha_1 \)**:
   - The smallest solution is \( x = \pi \).

7. **Calculate \( n\alpha_1 - ab \)**:
   - \( n = 3 \), \( \alpha_1 = \pi \), \( a = 3/2 \), \( b = -3\pi \).
   - \( n\alpha_1 - ab = 3\pi - (-9\pi/2) = 15\pi/2 \).
   - This gives \( q = 15 \) and \( p = 2 \), so \( p + q = 17 \).

Thus, the final answer is \(\boxed{17}\).
